<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Geography</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of lands and inhabitants of the Earth</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the science of the surfaces of the Earth, including the spatial interactions of its atmosphere, lithosphere, hydrosphere, biosphere, and human activity. For the surface of other planetary bodies, see <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>. For the science of planetary material, see <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">Geology</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Geography_(disambiguation)" title="Geography (disambiguation)">Geography (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Geo_(disambiguation)" title="Geo (disambiguation)">Geo</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Physical_World_Map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Physical_World_Map.svg/440px-Physical_World_Map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Physical_World_Map.svg/660px-Physical_World_Map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Physical_World_Map.svg/880px-Physical_World_Map.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Physical_World_Map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Physical map of Earth</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Map_(political).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/World_Map_%28political%29.svg/440px-World_Map_%28political%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/World_Map_%28political%29.svg/660px-World_Map_%28political%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/World_Map_%28political%29.svg/880px-World_Map_%28political%29.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Map_(political).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Political map of Earth</div></div></div>
<p><b>Geography</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>: <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γεωγραφία</span></span>, <i>geographia</i>, literally "earth description") is a field of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> devoted to the study of the lands, features, inhabitants, and phenomena of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro1-1">[1]</a></sup> The first recorded use of the word γεωγραφία was as a title of a book by Greek scholar <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> (276–194 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-intro1-1">[1]</a></sup> Geography is an all-encompassing discipline that seeks an understanding of Earth and <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">its human and natural complexities</a>—not merely where objects are, but also how they have changed and come to be. While geography is specific to Earth, many concepts can be applied more broadly to other celestial bodies in the field of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burt_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burt-2">[2]</a></sup> One such concept, the <a href="/wiki/Tobler%27s_first_law_of_geography" title="Tobler's first law of geography">first law of geography</a>, proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waldo_Tobler" title="Waldo Tobler">Waldo Tobler</a>, is "everything is related to everything else, but near things are more related than distant things."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler2-4">[4]</a></sup> Geography has been called "the world discipline" and "the bridge between the human and the <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_physical_science" title="Outline of physical science">physical sciences</a>". 
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span></h2>
<p>Geography is a systematic study of the Earth, its features, and phenomena that take place on it. For something to fall into the domain of geography, it generally needs some sort of spatial component that can be placed on a map, such as coordinates, place names, or addresses. This has led to geography being associated with <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> and place names. Although many geographers are trained in <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponymy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartology</a>, this is not their main preoccupation. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographers" title="Geographers">Geographers</a> study the Earth's spatial and temporal distribution of phenomena, processes, and features as well as the <a href="/wiki/Interaction" title="Interaction">interaction</a> of humans and their <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes1-5">[5]</a></sup> Because space and place affect a variety of topics, such as economics, health, <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, plants, and animals, geography is highly interdisciplinary. The interdisciplinary nature of the geographical approach depends on an attentiveness to the relationship between physical and human phenomena and their spatial patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hornby11_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hornby11-6">[6]</a></sup> Geography is specific to the planet Earth, and other celestial bodies are specified, such as "geography of Mars," or given another name, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mars" title="Geography of Mars">areography</a> in the case of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Names of places...are not geography...To know by heart a whole gazetteer full of them would not, in itself, constitute anyone a geographer. Geography has higher aims than this: it seeks to classify phenomena (alike of the natural and of the political world, in so far as it treats of the latter), to compare, to generalize, to ascend from effects to causes, and, in doing so, to trace out the laws of nature and to mark their influences upon man. This is 'a description of the world'—that is Geography. In a word, Geography is a Science—a thing not of mere names but of argument and reason, of cause and effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/William_Hughes_(geographer)" title="William Hughes (geographer)">William Hughes</a>, 1863</cite></div></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Just as all phenomena exist in time and thus have a history, they also exist in space and have a geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a>, 1997</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Geography as a discipline can be split broadly into three main subsidiary fields: <a href="/wiki/Human_geography" title="Human geography">human geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Technical_geography" title="Technical geography">technical geography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hough1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hough1-12">[12]</a></sup> The former largely focuses on the <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a> and how humans create, view, manage, and influence space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hough1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hough1-12">[12]</a></sup> The latter examines the natural environment, and how <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>, climate, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, water, and <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a> produce and interact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotterill_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotterill-13">[13]</a></sup> The difference between these approaches led to a third field, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_geography" title="Environmental geography">environmental geography</a>, which combines physical and human geography and concerns the interactions between the environment and humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes1-5">[5]</a></sup> Technical geography involves studying and developing the tools and techniques used by geographers, such as <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geographic_information_system" title="Geographic information system">geographic information system</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haidu1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haidu1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_concepts">Core concepts</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space">Space</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">Space</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_coordinate_system.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="487" data-file-width="487" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/3D_coordinate_system.svg/220px-3D_coordinate_system.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/3D_coordinate_system.svg/330px-3D_coordinate_system.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/3D_coordinate_system.svg/440px-3D_coordinate_system.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_coordinate_system.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A right-handed three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinate system</a> used to indicate positions in space.</div></div></div>
<p>For something to exist in the realm of geography, it must be able to be described spatially. Thus, space is the most fundamental concept at the foundation of geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrift1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thrift1-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1-16">[16]</a></sup> The concept is so basic, that geographers often have difficulty defining exactly what it is, and instead assume that everyone knows what they are talking about. At its most fundamental, <a href="/wiki/Absolute_space_and_time" title="Absolute space and time">absolute space</a> is the exact site, or spatial coordinates, of objects, persons, places, or phenomena under investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrift1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thrift1-15">[15]</a></sup> We exist in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuan1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuan1-17">[17]</a></sup> Absolute space leads to the view of the world as a photograph, with everything frozen in place when the coordinates were recorded. Today, geographers are trained to remember that the world is not the static image that appears on a map; and instead, the dynamic space where all processes interact and take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrift1_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thrift1-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Place">Place</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Location" title="Location">Location</a>, <a href="/wiki/Place_identity" title="Place identity">Place identity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_place" title="Sense of place">Sense of place</a></div>
<p>Place is one of the most complex terms in geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuan1_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tuan1-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castree1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castree1-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory1-19">[19]</a></sup> In human geography, place is the synthesis of the coordinates on the Earth's surface, the activity and use that occurs, has occurred, and will occur at the coordinates, and the meaning ascribed to the space by human individuals and groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castree1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castree1-18">[18]</a></sup> This can be extraordinarily complex, as different spaces may have different uses at different times and mean different things to different people. In physical geography, a place includes all of the physical phenomena that occur in space, including the lithosphere, atmosphere, hydrosphere, and biosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory1_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory1-19">[19]</a></sup> Places do not exist in a vacuum and instead have complex spatial relationships with each other, and place is concerned how a location is situated in relation to all other locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler2_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler2-4">[4]</a></sup> As a discipline then, the term place in geography includes all spatial phenomena occurring at a location, the diverse uses and meanings humans ascribe to that location, and how that location impacts and is impacted by all other locations on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castree1_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Castree1-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory1_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory1-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time">Time</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Time_geography" title="Time geography">Time geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historical_geography" title="Historical geography">Historical geography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png"><img alt="A space-time cube is a three-axis graph where one axis represents the time dimension and the other axes represent two spatial dimensions" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="499" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png/220px-Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png/330px-Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png/440px-Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sample_of_time_geographical_description.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Examples of the <a href="/wiki/Visual_language" title="Visual language">visual language</a> of time geography: space-time cube, path, prism, bundle, and other concepts.</div></div></div>
<p>Time is usually thought to be within the domain of history, however, it is of significant concern in the discipline of geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrift2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thrift2-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thornes1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thornes1-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2-22">[22]</a></sup> In physics, space and time are not separated, and are combined into the concept of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galison_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galison-23">[23]</a></sup>
Geography is subject to the laws of physics, and in studying things that occur in space, time must be considered. Time in geography is more than just the historical record of events that occurred at various discrete coordinates; but also includes modeling the dynamic movement of people, organisms, and things through space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuan1_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tuan1-17">[17]</a></sup> Time facilitates movement through space, ultimately allowing things to flow through a system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrift2_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thrift2-20">[20]</a></sup> The amount of time an individual, or group of people, spends in a place will often shape their attachment and perspective to that place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuan1_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tuan1-17">[17]</a></sup> Time constrains the possible paths that can be taken through space, given a starting point, possible routes, and rate of travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller1-24">[24]</a></sup> Visualizing time over space is challenging in terms of cartography, and includes Space-Prism and animated maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller1_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miller1-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scale">Scale</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scale_(ratio)" title="Scale (ratio)">Scale (ratio)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spatial_scale" title="Spatial scale">Spatial scale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scale_(map)" title="Scale (map)">Scale (map)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_scale_-_8km,_5mi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="125" data-file-width="397" decoding="async" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Map_scale_-_8km%2C_5mi.png/290px-Map_scale_-_8km%2C_5mi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Map_scale_-_8km%2C_5mi.png 1.5x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_scale_-_8km,_5mi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graphical or bar scale. A map would also usually give its scale numerically ("1:50,000", for instance, means that one cm on the map represents 50,000cm of real space, which is 500 meters)</div></div></div>
<p>Scale in the context of a map is the ratio between a distance measured on the map and the corresponding distance as measured on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burt_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burt-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herod_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herod-26">[26]</a></sup> This concept is fundamental to the discipline of geography, not just cartography, in that phenomena being investigated appear different depending on the scale used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arbia1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arbia1-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithLaws_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithLaws-28">[28]</a></sup> Scale is the frame that geographers use to measure space, and ultimately to try and understand a place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herod_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herod-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_of_Geography">Laws of Geography</span></h3>
<p>In general, some dispute the entire concept of laws in geography and the social sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler1_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler3_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler3-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodchild1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodchild1-30">[30]</a></sup> These criticisms have been addressed by Tobler and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler3_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler3-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodchild1_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodchild1-30">[30]</a></sup> However, this is an ongoing source of debate in geography and is unlikely to be resolved anytime soon. Several laws have been proposed, and Tobler's first law of geography is the most generally accepted in geography. Some have argued that geographic laws do not need to be numbered. The existence of a first invites a second, and many have proposed themselves as that. It has also been proposed that Tobler's first law of geography should be moved to the second and replaced with another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodchild1_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goodchild1-30">[30]</a></sup> A few of the proposed laws of geography are below:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tobler%27s_first_law_of_geography" title="Tobler's first law of geography">Tobler's first law of geography</a>: "Everything is related to everything else, but near things are more related than distant"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler1_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler3_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler3-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodchild1_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Goodchild1-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tobler%27s_second_law_of_geography" title="Tobler's second law of geography">Tobler's second law of geography</a>: "the phenomenon external to a geographic area of interest affects what goes on inside."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler3_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler3-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arbia%27s_law_of_geography" title="Arbia's law of geography">Arbia's law of geography</a>: "Everything is related to everything else, but things observed at a coarse spatial resolution are more related than things observed at a finer resolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arbia1_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arbia1-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobler3_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tobler3-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithLaws_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-SmithLaws-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_uncertainty_principle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The uncertainty principle (page does not exist)">the uncertainty principle</a>: "that the geographic world is infinitely complex and that any representation must therefore contain elements of uncertainty, that many definitions used in acquiring geographic data contain elements of vagueness, and that it is impossible to measure location on the Earth's surface exactly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodchild1_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Goodchild1-30">[30]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-disciplines">Sub-disciplines</span></h2>
<p>Geography is a branch of inquiry that focuses on spatial information on Earth. It is an extremely broad topic and can be broken down multiple ways. There have been several approaches to doing this, including "four traditions of geography" and into distinct branches. The Four traditions of geography are often used to divide the different historical approaches geographers have taken to the discipline. In contrast, the branches of geography are used to describe contemporary geographers' approaches. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_traditions_of_geography">Four traditions of geography</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:Four_traditions_of_geography" title="Category:Four traditions of geography">Category:Four traditions of geography</a></div>
<p>Geography is an extremely broad field. Because of this, many view the various definitions of geography proposed over the decades as inadequate. To address this, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/William_Patterson" title="William Patterson">William Patterson</a> proposed the concept of the "Four traditions of Geography" in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup> These traditions are the Spatial or Locational Tradition, the Man-Land or Human-Environment Interaction Tradition, the Area Studies or Regional Tradition, and the Earth Science Tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup> These concepts are broad sets of geography philosophies bound together within the discipline. They are one of many ways geographers organize the major sets of thoughts and philosophies within the discipline. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spatial_or_locational_tradition">Spatial or locational tradition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spatial_Analysis" title="Spatial Analysis">Spatial Analysis</a></div>
<p>The spatial or locational tradition is concerned with employing quantitative methods to describe the spatial characteristics of a location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup> The spatial tradition seeks to use the spatial characteristics of a location or phenomena to understand and explain it. The contributors to this tradition were historically cartographers, but it now encompasses what we call technical geography and geographic information science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Area_studies_or_regional_tradition">Area studies or regional tradition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regional_geography" title="Regional geography">Regional geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Area_studies" title="Area studies">Area studies</a></div>
<p>The area studies or regional tradition is concerned with the description of the unique characteristics of the earth's surface, resulting in each area from the combination of its complete natural or elements, as of physical and human environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minshull1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minshull1-34">[34]</a></sup> The main aim is to understand, or define the uniqueness, or character of a particular region that consists of natural as well as human elements. Attention is paid also to <a href="/wiki/Regionalisation" title="Regionalisation">regionalization</a>, which covers the proper techniques of space <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delimitation" title="Delimitation">delimitation</a> into regions.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human-Environment_interaction_tradition">Human-Environment interaction tradition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Integrated_geography" title="Integrated geography">Integrated geography</a></div>
<p>The Human Environment Interaction tradition (originally the Man-Land), also known as <a href="/wiki/Integrated_geography" title="Integrated geography">Integrated geography</a>, is concerned with the description of the spatial interactions between humans and the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang1-35">[35]</a></sup> It requires an understanding of the traditional aspects of physical and human geography, like how human societies conceptualize the environment. Integrated geography has emerged as a bridge between human and physical geography due to the increasing specialization of the two sub-fields. Since the changing of the human relationship with the environment as a result of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technological_change" title="Technological change">technological change</a>, a new approach was needed to understand the changing and dynamic relationship. Examples of areas of research in environmental geography include: <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">emergency management</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_management" title="Environmental management">environmental management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_ecology" title="Political ecology">political ecology</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_science_tradition">Earth science tradition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth science</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth science</a> tradition is largely concerned with what is generally referred to as physical geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions1_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions2_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions2-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup> The tradition focuses on understanding the spatial characteristics of natural phenomena. Some argue the Earth science tradition is a subset of the spatial tradition, however the two are different enough in their focus and objectives to warrant separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traditions3_33-8"><a href="#cite_note-Traditions3-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches_of_geography">Branches of geography</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:Branches_of_geography" title="Category:Branches of geography">Category:Branches of geography</a></div>
<p>Research and careers in geography today are highly multidisciplinary. Geography is often organized into three branches: <a href="/wiki/Human_geography" title="Human geography">human geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Technical_geography" title="Technical geography">technical geography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haidu1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haidu1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro1_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-intro1-1">[1]</a></sup> Geographers rarely focus on just one of these topics, often using one as their primary focus and then incorporating data and methods from the other branches. Geographers are more likely to identify closely with a specific branch when describing themselves to lay people than with grand traditions. Human geography is concerned with studying people and their communities, cultures, economies, and interactions with the environment by studying their relations with and across space and place. Physical geography is concerned with the study of processes and patterns in the natural environment like the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geosphere" title="Geosphere">geosphere</a>. Technical geography is interested in studying and applying techniques and methods to store, process, analyze, visualize, and use spatial data. These branches use similar geographic philosophies, concepts, and tools and often overlap significantly. 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">Physical geography</a></div>
<p>Physical geography (or physiography) focuses on geography as an <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> It aims to understand the physical problems and the issues of <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pedosphere" title="Pedosphere">pedosphere</a>, and global <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> patterns (<a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>). Physical geography is the study of earth's seasons, <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, streams, landforms, and oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockyer1_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockyer1-39">[39]</a></sup> Physical geographers will often work in identifying and monitoring the use of natural resources.  
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerycaption">Physical geography can be divided into many broad categories, including:</li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human">Human</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_geography" title="Human geography">Human geography</a></div>
<p>Human geography (or anthropogeography) is a branch of geography that focuses on studying patterns and processes that shape human society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HGDICT_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-HGDICT-40">[40]</a></sup> It encompasses the human, political, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a>, social, and economic aspects. In industry, human geographers often work in city planning, public health, or business analysis. 
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<p>Various approaches to the study of human geography have also arisen through time and include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Behavioral_geography" title="Behavioral geography">Behavioral geography</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Culture_theory" title="Culture theory">Culture theory</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Feminist_geography" title="Feminist geography">Feminist geography</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Geosophy" title="Geosophy">Geosophy</a></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical">Technical</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Technical_geography" title="Technical geography">Technical geography</a></div>
<p>Technical geography concerns studying and developing tools, techniques, and statistical methods employed to collect, analyze, use, and understand spatial data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haidu1_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Haidu1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ormeling1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ormeling1-41">[41]</a></sup> Technical geography is the most recently recognized of the branches. As academic fields increasingly specialize in their nature, technical geography has emerged as a branch of geography specializing in geographic methods and thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haidu1_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Haidu1-14">[14]</a></sup> The emergence of technical geography has brought new relevance to the broad discipline of geography by serving as a set of unique methods for managing the interdisciplinary nature of the discipline. While human and physical geographers use the techniques employed by technical geographers, technical geography is more concerned with the fundamental spatial concepts and technologies than the nature of the data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haidu1_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Haidu1-14">[14]</a></sup> A technical geographer might work as a GIS analyst or create general reference maps incorporating human and natural features. 
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerycaption">Technical geography can be divided into many broad categories, such as:</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:22px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Cartographic_Process.png" title="Cartography"><img alt="Cartography" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="810" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/The_Cartographic_Process.png/120px-The_Cartographic_Process.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/The_Cartographic_Process.png/180px-The_Cartographic_Process.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/The_Cartographic_Process.png/240px-The_Cartographic_Process.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_fields">Related fields</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">Planetary science</a>: While the discipline of geography is normally concerned with the Earth, the term can also be informally used to describe the study of other worlds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and even beyond. The study of systems larger than the Earth itself usually forms part of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">Astronomy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">Cosmology</a>. The study of other planets is usually called <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>. Alternative terms such as <a href="/wiki/Areography_(geography_of_Mars)" title="Areography (geography of Mars)">areography (geography of Mars)</a> have been employed to describe the study of other celestial objects. Ultimately, geography may be considered a subdiscipline within planetary science.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques">Techniques</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x,
